<p><b>Harakat al-Shabaab al-Mujahideen</b> (<b>HSM</b>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">حركة الشباب المجاهدين</span>‎‎, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">Ḥarakat ash-Shabāb al-Mujāhidīn</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a>: <span lang="so" xml:lang="so">Xarakada Mujaahidiinta Alshabaab</span>, lit. "<a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">Mujahideen</a> Youth Movement" or "Movement of Striving Youth"), more commonly known as <b>Al-Shabaab</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">الشباب</span>‎, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'"The Youth" or "The Youngsters"'‎), is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafism" title="Salafism">Salafist</a> <a href="/wiki/Jihadism" title="Jihadism">jihadist</a> <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">fundamentalist group</a> based in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>. In 2012, it pledged allegiance to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Militant_Islamism" title="Militant Islamism">militant Islamist</a> organization <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlQaeda_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlQaeda-7">[7]</a></sup> In February of the year, some of the group's leaders quarreled with Al-Qaeda over the union,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and quickly lost ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Al-Shabaab's troop strength was estimated at 7,000 to 9,000 militants in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jgagvfts_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jgagvfts-5">[5]</a></sup> As of 2015, the group has retreated from the major cities, controlling a few rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uptpibais_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uptpibais-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Al-Shabaab is an offshoot of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Courts_Union" title="Islamic Courts Union">Islamic Courts Union</a> (ICU), which splintered into several smaller factions after its defeat in 2006 by Somalia's <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Government" title="Transitional Federal Government">Transitional Federal Government</a> (TFG) and the TFG's Ethiopian military allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abdisaid2008_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abdisaid2008-12">[12]</a></sup> The group describes itself as waging jihad against "enemies of Islam", and is engaged in combat against the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Government_of_Somalia" title="Federal Government of Somalia">Federal Government of Somalia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/African_Union_Mission_to_Somalia" title="African Union Mission to Somalia">African Union Mission to Somalia</a> (AMISOM). Al-Shabaab has been designated as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrorist_organization" title="Terrorist organization">terrorist organization</a> by Australia, Canada, the United Arab Emirates, the United Kingdom and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dos_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-dos-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPSS_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPSS-14">[14]</a></sup> As of June 2012, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">US State Department</a> has open bounties on several of the group's senior commanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smsascrfboa_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smsascrfboa-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early August 2011, the Transitional Federal Government's troops and their AMISOM allies managed to capture all of Mogadishu from the Al-Shabaab militants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rsgdird_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rsgdird-16">[16]</a></sup> An ideological rift within the group's leadership also emerged, and several of the organization's senior commanders were assassinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Csfdafbtas_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Csfdafbtas-17">[17]</a></sup> Due to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahhabi" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a> roots, Al Shabaab is hostile to <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and has often clashed with the militant Sufi group <a href="/wiki/Ahlu_Sunna_Waljama%27a" title="Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a">Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The group has also been suspected of having links with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_Islamic_Maghreb" title="Al-Qaeda in Islamic Maghreb">Al-Qaeda in Islamic Maghreb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram" title="Boko Haram">Boko Haram</a>. Additionally, it attracted some members from western countries, notably <a href="/wiki/Samantha_Lewthwaite" title="Samantha Lewthwaite">Samantha Lewthwaite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansoor_Al-Amriki" title="Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki">Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki</a>.</p>
<p>In August 2014, the Somali government-led <a href="/wiki/Operation_Indian_Ocean" title="Operation Indian Ocean">Operation Indian Ocean</a> was launched to clean up the remaining insurgent-held pockets in the countryside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spsgdncfmaep_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spsgdncfmaep-20">[20]</a></sup> On 1 September 2014, a U.S. drone strike carried out as part of the broader mission killed Al-Shabaab leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moktar_Ali_Zubeyr" title="Moktar Ali Zubeyr">Moktar Ali Zubeyr</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pcdostl_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pcdostl-21">[21]</a></sup> U.S. authorities hailed the raid as a major symbolic and operational loss for Al-Shabaab, and the Somali government offered a 45-day amnesty to all moderate members of the militant group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ucdostgl_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ucdostgl-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Al-Shabaab is also known as <b>Ash-Shabaab</b>, <b>Hizbul Shabaab</b> ("Party of the Youth"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WASHINGTONS-SELF-DEFEATING-SOMALIA-POLICY_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-WASHINGTONS-SELF-DEFEATING-SOMALIA-POLICY-23">[23]</a></sup> Popular Resistance Movement in the Land of the Two Migrations (PRM) (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">حركة المقاومة الشعبية فى بلاد الهجرتين</span>‎‎) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DIPLOMATS-STRESS-NEED-FOR-ALL-INCLUSIVE-TALKS_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-DIPLOMATS-STRESS-NEED-FOR-ALL-INCLUSIVE-TALKS-24">[24]</a></sup> For short, the organization is referred to as <b>HSM</b>, which stands for "Harakat al-Shabaab al-Mujahideen". The term <i>Shabaab</i> means "youth" in Arabic, and the group should not be confused with <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(disambiguation)" title="Al-Shabaab (disambiguation)">similarly</a> named groups.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_leadership">Organization and leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Organization and leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">Mujahideen</a></div>
<p>Al-Shabaab's composition is multiethnic, with its leadership positions mainly occupied by <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>-trained ethnic Somalis and foreigners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fastttcis_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fastttcis-25">[25]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Counterterrorism_Center" title="National Counterterrorism Center">National Counterterrorism Center</a>, the group's rank-and-file members hail from disparate local groups, sometimes recruited by force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asnctc_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asnctc-26">[26]</a></sup> Unlike most of the organization's top leaders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cacisasatmotcp_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cacisasatmotcp-27">[27]</a></sup> its foot soldiers are primarily concerned with nationalist and clan-related affairs as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">global jihad</a>. They are also prone to infighting and shifting alliances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asnctc_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asnctc-26">[26]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Jamestown_Foundation" title="Jamestown Foundation">Jamestown Foundation</a>, Al-Shabaab seeks to exploit these vulnerabilities by manipulating clan networks in order to retain power. The group itself is likewise not entirely immune to local politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cacisasatmotcp_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cacisasatmotcp-27">[27]</a></sup> More recently, Muslim converts from neighbouring countries have been conscripted, typically to do undesirable or difficult work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anbotib_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anbotib-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although al-Shabaab's leadership ultimately falls upon al-Qaeda leader <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ameer_Ragheb&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ameer Ragheb (page does not exist)">Ameer Ragheb</a>, the internal leadership is not fully clear, and with foreign fighters trickling out of the country, its structure is increasingly decentralized. Ahmed Abdi Godane was publicly named as emir of al-Shabaab in December 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In August 2011, Godane was heavily criticized by Al-Shabaab co-founder Hassan Dahir Aweys and others for not letting aid into the hunger stricken parts of southern Somalia. Although not formally announced, Shabaab was effectively split up into a "foreign legion," led by Godane, and a coalition of factions forming a "national legion" under Aweys. The latter group often refused to take orders from Godane and the two groups hardly talked to each other. In February 2012, Godane made <a href="/wiki/Bay%27ah" title="Bay'ah">Bay'ah</a>, or an oath of allegiance, to al-Qaeda. With it he likely hoped to reclaim and extend his authority, and to encourage foreign fighters to stay. This move will further complicate the cooperation with the "national legion" of al-Shabaab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlQaeda_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-AlQaeda-7">[7]</a></sup> Godane was killed in a U.S. drone strike in Somalia on September 1, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edition.cnn.com_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-edition.cnn.com-30">[30]</a></sup> Ahmad Umar was named Godane's successor on 6 September 2014, he is believed to have previously played a role in al-Shabaab's internal secret service known as <i>Amniya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ubaidah_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ubaidah-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaders">Leaders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Leaders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Umar" title="Ahmad Umar">Ahmad Umar</a> (Abu Ubaidah) (2014–)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ubaidah_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ubaidah-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moktar_Ali_Zubeyr" title="Moktar Ali Zubeyr">Moktar Ali Zubeyr "Godane"</a> (2007–2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> – Arab sub-clan of northern <a href="/wiki/Isaaq" title="Isaaq">Isaaq</a> clan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clansuna_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-clansuna-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Srsisimr_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Srsisimr-34">[34]</a></sup> (killed in U.S. drone strike in 2014)</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Other leaders:</b></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muktar_robow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="170" data-file-width="130" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/73/Muktar_robow.jpg" width="130"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muktar_robow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_Robow" title="Mukhtar Robow">Mukhtar Robow</a> ("Abu Mansoor"), the Second Deputy Leader of Al-Shabaab.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_Robow" title="Mukhtar Robow">Mukhtar Robow "Abu Mansoor"</a> – Second Deputy Leader and regional commander in charge of Bay and Bakool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuad_Mohamed_Khalaf" title="Fuad Mohamed Khalaf">Fuad Mohammed Khalaf "Shangole"</a> – third-most important leader after "Abu Mansoor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCSOM_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCSOM-36">[36]</a></sup> In charge of public affairs. (Awrtabe sub-clan of <a href="/wiki/Darod" title="Darod">Darod</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clansuna_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-clansuna-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hassan_Dahir_Aweys" title="Hassan Dahir Aweys">Hassan Dahir Aweys</a> – spiritual leader<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> (surrendered to Federal Government in 2013.)</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hussein_Ali_Fidow&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hussein Ali Fidow (page does not exist)">Hussein Ali Fidow</a> – political chief and Wasiir (Prime Minister)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ali Mohamud Raghe "Dheere" aka <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sheikh_Ali_Mohamud_Rage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sheikh Ali Mohamud Rage (page does not exist)">Sheikh Ali Mohamud Rage</a> aka Sheikh Ali Dhere – current Deputy Amir. He is from <a href="/wiki/Hawiye" title="Hawiye">Hawiye</a> Murusade clan. Official spokesman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCSOM_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCSOM-36">[36]</a></sup> (Not to be confused with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheikh_Ali_Dhere" title="Sheikh Ali Dhere">Sheikh Ali Dhere</a> who established the first Islamic court in Mogadishu in 1996).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aden_Hashi_Farah" title="Aden Hashi Farah">Aden Hashi Farah "Ayro"</a> – central <a href="/wiki/Hawiye" title="Hawiye">Hawiye</a> clan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trafomislc_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trafomislc-39">[39]</a></sup> (killed in U.S. airstrike in 2008.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Haji_Jama_Mee%27aad" title="Ibrahim Haji Jama Mee'aad">Ibrahim Haji Jama Mee'aad "al-Afghani"</a> (Abubakar al-Seyli'i) – He was Governor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kisimayo" title="Kisimayo">Kisimayo</a> administration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCSOM_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCSOM-36">[36]</a></sup> (killed by Godane loyalists in 2013.)</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hassan_Yaqub_Ali&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hassan Yaqub Ali (page does not exist)">Hassan Yaqub Ali</a> – was official spokesman of the Kisimayo administration but currently he is Waali (governor) of Gal-Mudug.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCSOM_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCSOM-36">[36]</a></sup> (Rahanwayn clan)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clansuna_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-clansuna-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Abdirahman_Hassan_Hussein&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Abdirahman Hassan Hussein (page does not exist)">Abdirahman Hassan Hussein</a> – leader (Governor) of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shabelleh_Dhexe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shabelleh Dhexe (page does not exist)">Middle Shabelle</a> region<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hassan_Abdullah_Hersi_al-Turki" title="Hassan Abdullah Hersi al-Turki">Hassan Abdullah Hersi "al-Turki"</a> – leader of the <a href="/wiki/Ras_Kamboni_Brigades" title="Ras Kamboni Brigades">Ras Kamboni Brigades</a>, which controls the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juba_Valley" title="Juba Valley">Juba Valley</a> and was first part of <a href="/wiki/Hizbul_Islam" title="Hizbul Islam">Hizbul Islam</a> but merged with al-Shabaab in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> (Ogaden sub-clan of <a href="/wiki/Darod" title="Darod">Darod</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clansuna_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-clansuna-33">[33]</a></sup> (Died of natural causes in 2014.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Said_Atom" title="Mohamed Said Atom">Mohamed Said Atom</a> – faction leader and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arms_dealer" title="Arms dealer">arms dealer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> who in July 2010 announced allegiance to al-Shabaab and the al-Shabaab commander in <a href="/wiki/Puntland" title="Puntland">Puntland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes20100809_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes20100809-44">[44]</a></sup> (surrendered to Federal Government in 2014.)</li>
<li>Mukhtar Abu-Muslim – head of fatwas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alqaeda_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-alqaeda-45">[45]</a></sup> from Rahanweyn clan.</li>
<li>Abdulahi Haji "Daud"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alqaeda_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-alqaeda-45">[45]</a></sup> – head of assassinations, from <a href="/wiki/Hawiye" title="Hawiye">Hawiye</a> clan of Murursade sub-clan.</li>
<li>Sahal Isku Dhuuq<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alqaeda_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-alqaeda-45">[45]</a></sup> – head of kidnappings of aid workers for ransom, from Dir clan of <a href="/wiki/Bimaal" title="Bimaal">Bimaal</a> sub-clan.</li>
<li>Hassan Afrah,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alqaeda_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-alqaeda-45">[45]</a></sup> – head of relationship with pirates, from <a href="/wiki/Hawiye" title="Hawiye">Hawiye</a> clan of Saleban sub-clan.</li>
<li>Dahir Gamaey "Abdi Al-Haq"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alqaeda_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-alqaeda-45">[45]</a></sup> – judge of Al-Shabaab, from <a href="/wiki/Hawiye" title="Hawiye">Hawiye</a> clan of Duduble sub-clan.</li>
<li>Tahliil Abdishakur – head of the elite Al-Amniyat assassination unit (killed in U.S. drone strike in 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ucdskalis_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ucdskalis-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li>Yusuf Dheeq – chief of external operations and planning for intelligence and security (killed in U.S. drone strike in 2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gsgwtdoaslyd_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gsgwtdoaslyd-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>Aden Garaar – head of external operations of Al-Shabaab; reportedly orchestrated the 2013 Westgate shopping mall attack in Nairobi (killed in U.S. drone strike in 2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ucosistal_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ucosistal-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li>Mohamed Musa – Gedo province commander (killed in skirmish with Somali army in 2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gctgfgkarl_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gctgfgkarl-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sheikh_Abdiasis_Abu_Musab&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sheikh Abdiasis Abu Musab (page does not exist)">Sheikh Abdiasis Abu Musab</a> - military operations spokesman</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreigners">Foreigners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Foreigners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>al-Shabaab is said to have many foreigners within its ranks, particularly at the leadership level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fastttcis_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fastttcis-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Fighters from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and international jihadists were called to join the holy war against the Somali government and its Ethiopian allies. Though Somali Islamists did not originally use suicide bombing tactics, the foreign elements of al-Shabaab have been blamed for several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suicide_bombing" title="Suicide bombing">suicide bombings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SanDiegoUnionTribune_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-SanDiegoUnionTribune-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> A 2006 UN report identified <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, among countries in the region, as the main backers of the Islamist extremists. Egypt has a longstanding policy of securing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a> flow by destabilizing Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Formerly a predominantly nationalist organization, al-Shabaab repositioned itself as a militant Islamist group that also attracted a large cadre of Western devotees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> As of 2011, the group's foreign recruitment strategy was active in the United States, where members attempted to recruit from the local Muslim communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aqgasrmaur_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aqgasrmaur-56">[56]</a></sup> According to an investigative report by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._House_Committee_on_Homeland_Security" title="U.S. House Committee on Homeland Security">U.S. House Committee on Homeland Security</a>, Al Shabaab recruited over 40 Muslim Americans since 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aqgasrmaur_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aqgasrmaur-56">[56]</a></sup> In 2010, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> reported that after more than a dozen Americans were killed in Somalia, the organization's recruiting success had decreased in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes2-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>These American and foreign recruits played a dual role within the organization, serving as mercenaries and as a propaganda tool for radicalization and recruitment. These individuals, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omar_Hammami" title="Omar Hammami">Omar Hammami</a>, appeared in propaganda videos posted in online forums to appeal to disaffected Muslim youth and inspire them to join the Islamist struggle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al_Shabaab_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Al_Shabaab-58">[58]</a></sup> This was a top-down strategy, wherein Islamist agents attempted to use mosques and legitimate businesses as a cover to meet, recruit, and raise funds for operations in the US and abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al_Shabaab_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Al_Shabaab-58">[58]</a></sup> By mid-2013, the U.S. Congress reported that such militant recruitment appeared to have halted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mrosayahsuc_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mrosayahsuc-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of the foreign al-Shabaab members come from <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>. As of 2010, their number was estimated at between 200 and 300 militants, augmented by around 1,000 diasporan ethnic Somalis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fastttcis_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fastttcis-25">[25]</a></sup> Many of Al-Shabaab's foot soldiers also belong to Somalia's marginalized ethnic minorities from the farming south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the foreign members, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jonathan_Evans_(MI5_officer)" title="Jonathan Evans (MI5 officer)">Jonathan Evans</a>, the former head of <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">MI5</a>, addressing a London security conference in 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> advised that "a significant number of UK residents" were training with al-Shabaab. Linking this increased involvement with a reduction in Al Qaida activity in Pakistan's tribal areas, he also suggested that since Somalia, like Afghanistan, at the time had no effective central government, the presence of foreign fighters there could inspire terrorist incidents in the UK. "It is only a matter of time before we see terrorism on our streets inspired by those who are today fighting alongside al-Shabaab."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burleigh_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burleigh-62">[62]</a></sup> The actual number has been estimated at between 50<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and 100<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ttbfanwotamw_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ttbfanwotamw-64">[64]</a></sup> persons; one source estimating around 60 active Al-Shabaab recruiters, including 40 Somalis and an additional 20 mainly British-based '<a href="/wiki/Cleanskin_(security)" title="Cleanskin (security)">clean skins</a>', individuals who have not committed any crimes but are believed to have ties with the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mfossjpiuk_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mfossjpiuk-65">[65]</a></sup> There is also evidence of funding of the group by Somalis resident in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burleigh_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burleigh-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chorley_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chorley-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the ten people subject to <a href="/wiki/Control_order" title="Control order">control orders</a> (now <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Terrorism_Prevention_and_Investigation_Measures_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Terrorism Prevention and Investigation Measures Act (page does not exist)">Tpim orders</a>) in 2012, at least five are associated with al-Shabaab: (pseudonymously) CC, CE "a British citizen of Iranian origin, aged 28 in 2012", CF, and DD "a non-British citizen […] believed […] to have been associated with the funding and promotion of [terrorism-related activity] in East Africa."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> At least two British Somalis, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ibrahim_Magag&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ibrahim Magag (page does not exist)">Ibrahim Magag</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chorley_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chorley-66">[66]</a></sup> (referred to as BX in Court documentation) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mohammed_Ahmed_Mohamed&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mohammed Ahmed Mohamed (page does not exist)">Mohammed Ahmed Mohamed</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> have absconded.</p>
<p>In 2012, it was also reported that the group was attracting an increasing number of non-Somali recent converts from <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, a predominantly Christian country in the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region. Estimates in 2014 placed the figure of Kenyan fighters at around 25% of Al-Shabaab's total forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fjacliyk_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fjacliyk-69">[69]</a></sup> Referred to as the "Kenyan Mujahideen" by Al-Shabaab's core members,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anbotib_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anbotib-28">[28]</a></sup> the converts are typically young and overzealous. Poverty has made them easier targets for the group's recruiting activities. The Kenyan insurgents can blend in with the general population of Kenya, and they are often harder to track by law enforcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenya_and_the_Global_War_on_Terror_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenya_and_the_Global_War_on_Terror-70">[70]</a></sup> Reports suggest that al-Shabaab is attempting to build an even more multi-ethnic generation of fighters in the larger region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rspiaamgga_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rspiaamgga-71">[71]</a></sup> One such recent convert, who helped carry out the <a href="/wiki/Kampala" title="Kampala">Kampala</a> bombings but now cooperates with the Kenyan police, believes that the group is trying to use local Kenyans to do its "dirty work" for it, while its own core members escape unscathed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anbotib_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Anbotib-28">[28]</a></sup> According to diplomats, Muslim areas in coastal Kenya and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>, are especially vulnerable for recruitment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rspiaamgga_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rspiaamgga-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Foreigners from Afghanistan and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, as well as Afghan-trained Somalis, play an important role in the group's leadership ranks owing to their combat experience. Bringing with them specialized skills, these commanders often lead the indoctrination of new recruits, and provide training in remote-controlled roadside bombings, suicide attack techniques, and the assassination and kidnapping of government officials, journalists, humanitarian and civil society workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fastttcis_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fastttcis-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Foreign al-Shabaab commanders include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Foreign leaders and members:</b></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jehad_Serwan_Mostafa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="170" data-file-width="130" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Jehad_Serwan_Mostafa.jpg" width="130"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jehad_Serwan_Mostafa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jehad Serwan Mostafa ("Emir Anwar"), a senior Al-Shabaab commander and trainer.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fazul_Abdullah_Mohammed" title="Fazul Abdullah Mohammed">Fazul Abdullah Mohammed</a>: Mohammed, a Kenyan national, was appointed by Osama bin Laden as Al-Qaeda's leader in East Africa in late 2009. Before the death of Saleh Ali Saleh Nabhan, Mohammed served as the military operations chief for Al-Qaeda in the region. He was an experienced militant commander who was known to be able to cross national borders with ease. In August 2008, he eluded a police dragnet in Kenya. Mohammed had been hiding in Somalia with Shabaab and the Islamic Courts for years. Mohammed was considered Al-Shabaab's military leader, while Muktar Abdelrahman Abu Zubeyr was Al-Shabaab's spiritual leader. He was killed on June 8, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widening_vacuum_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Widening_vacuum-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>Jehad Serwan Mostafa (alias "Ahmed Gurey", "Anwar al-Amriki" and "Emir Anwar"): a US-born senior Al-Shabaab commander. In charge of various functions for the militant group, including serving as a leader for foreign fighters within the organization as well as training insurgents. Fluent in English, Somali and Arabic, he is also a media specialist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jsmo_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jsmo-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li>Shaykh Muhammad Abu Fa'id: Fai'd, a Saudi citizen, serves as a top financier and a "manager" for Shabaab.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abu_Musa_Mombasa" title="Abu Musa Mombasa">Abu Musa Mombasa</a>: Mombasa, a Pakistani citizen, serves as Shabaab's chief of security and training.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansoor_Al-Amriki" title="Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki">Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki</a>: Amriki, whose real name was Omar Hammami, was a U.S. citizen who converted to Islam and traveled to Somalia in 2006. Once in Somalia, he quickly rose through the ranks. He served as a military commander, recruiter, financier, and propagandist. Amriki appeared in several Shabaab propaganda tapes. He became a primary recruiter for Al Shabaab; issued written statements on their behalf and appeared in its propaganda videos and audio recordings. An indictment unsealed in August 2010 charged him with providing material support to terrorists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In January 2013, Amriki was ousted from al-Shabaab because it felt he had joined in a "narcissistic pursuit of fame". He then publicly voiced ideological differences with the group via <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>, asserting that local militant leaders were only concerned with fighting in Somalia and not globally. He was assassinated by the insurgents in September 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rajkr_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rajkr-76">[76]</a></sup> He was removed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a>'s <a href="/wiki/FBI_Most_Wanted_Terrorists" title="FBI Most Wanted Terrorists">Most Wanted Terrorists list</a> in November 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> He was removed from the US State Department's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rewards_for_Justice" title="Rewards for Justice">Rewards for Justice</a> list in January 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abdulkadir_Mohamed_Abdulkadir.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="170" data-file-width="130" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Abdulkadir_Mohamed_Abdulkadir.jpg" width="130"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abdulkadir_Mohamed_Abdulkadir.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abdulkadir Mohamed Abdulkadir ("Ikrima"), a senior Al-Shabaab regional commander.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ikrima" title="Ikrima">Abdulkadir Mohamed Abdulkadir ("Ikrima")</a>: a Kenya-born Somali Al-Shabaab commander alleged by the Kenyan government to have planned several attacks in the country, including a plot to target the UN's bureau in Nairobi, the Kenyan parliamentary building, and an Ethiopian restaurant patronized by Somali government representatives. According to US officials, Abdulkadir was also a close associate of the late Al-Qaeda operatives Harun Fazul and Saleh Nabhan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Usrstascio_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Usrstascio-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sstnaiaqraam_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sstnaiaqraam-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li>Mahmud Mujajir: Mujajir, a Sudanese citizen, is Shabaab's chief of recruitment for suicide bombers.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Samantha_Lewthwaite" title="Samantha Lewthwaite">Samantha Lewthwaite</a>: Allegedly an Al-Shabaab member, she is believed to have been behind an attack on a sports bar in Mombasa in 2012. Widow of 7/7 suicide bomber <a href="/wiki/Germaine_Lindsay" title="Germaine Lindsay">Germaine Lindsay</a>.</li>
<li>Issa Osman Issa: Issa serves as a top al-Qaeda recruiter and military strategist for Shabaab. Before joining, he participated in the simultaneous attacks on the U.S. embassies in Nairobi and <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a> in 1998. He has been described as a central player in the simultaneous attacks on the Paradise Hotel in Kikambala, Kenya, in 2002, and the attempt that year to down an Israeli airliner in <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oath_of_Loyalty_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oath_of_Loyalty-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Significant_Role_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Significant_Role-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Mohamed Mohamud, also known as Sheikh Dulayadayn, Gamadhere or Mohamed Kuno, a Kenyan citizen of Somali origin who served as a commander of Al-Shabaab operations in Kenya. Named by the Kenyan government as the mastermind behind the <a href="/wiki/Garissa_University_College_attack" title="Garissa University College attack">Garissa University College attack</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asswkpolgw_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asswkpolgw-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> He was killed alongside 16 other militants in an overnight raid by Somali forces on June 1, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terrorist_designation">Terrorist designation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Terrorist designation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Countries and organizations below have officially listed Al-Shabaab as a terrorist organization.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_activities">History and activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History and activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/War_in_Somalia_(2006%E2%80%932009)" title="War in Somalia (2006–2009)">War in Somalia (2006–2009)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Somalia_(2009%E2%80%93)" title="War in Somalia (2009–)">War in Somalia (2009–)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Al-Shabaab_related_events" title="Timeline of Al-Shabaab related events">Timeline of Al-Shabaab related events</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3169" data-file-width="3000" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png/220px-Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png/330px-Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png/440px-Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Somalia_map_states_regions_districts.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Political situation in Somalia as of October 12, 2014.</div>
</div>
</div>
