<div id="Somali_Civil_War" style="font-size:114%"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">Somali Civil War</a></span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Somali_Rebellion" title="Somali Rebellion">Somali Rebellion</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Unified_Task_Force" title="Unified Task Force">Unified Task Force</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Consolidation_of_states_within_Somalia_(1998%E2%80%932006)" title="Consolidation of states within Somalia (1998–2006)">Consolidation of states within Somalia (1998–2006)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Advance_of_the_Islamic_Courts_Union" title="Advance of the Islamic Courts Union">Advance of the Islamic Courts Union</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Somalia_(2006%E2%80%9309)" title="War in Somalia (2006–09)">War in Somalia (2006–09)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/War_in_Somalia_(2009%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Somalia (2009–present)">War in Somalia (2009–present)</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>While Al-Shabaab previously represented the hard-line militant youth movement within the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Courts_Union" title="Islamic Courts Union">Islamic Courts Union</a> (ICU),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> it is now described as an extremist splinter group of the ICU. Since the ICU's downfall, however, the distinction between the youth movement and the so-called successor organization to the ICU, the PRM, appears to have been blurred. Al-Shabaab had recently begun encouraging people from across society, including elders, to join their ranks. In February 2012, Fu'ad Mohamed Khalaf Shongole, the chief of awareness raising of al-Shabaab, said that "At this stage of the jihad, fathers and mothers must send their unmarried girls to fight alongside the (male) militants". The addition of elders and young girls marks a change in the movement, which had previously involved only men, particularly young boys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASWGTJWAG_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASWGTJWAG-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Their core consisted of veterans who had fought and defeated the secular Mogadishu faction leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_the_Restoration_of_Peace_and_Counter-Terrorism" title="Alliance for the Restoration of Peace and Counter-Terrorism">Alliance for the Restoration of Peace and Counter-Terrorism</a> (ARPCT) at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Mogadishu" title="Second Battle of Mogadishu">Second Battle of Mogadishu</a>. Their origins are not clearly known, but former members say Hizbul Shabaab was founded as early as 2004. The membership of Al-Shabaab also includes various foreign fighters from around the world, according to an Islamic hardliner Mukhtar Robow "Abu Manssor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2009, Ethiopian forces withdrew from Somalia and Al-Shabaab carried on its fight against former ally and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Courts_Union" title="Islamic Courts Union">Islamic Courts Union</a> leader, President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharif_Ahmed" title="Sharif Ahmed">Sharif Ahmed</a>, who was the head of the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Federal_Parliament" title="Transitional Federal Parliament">Transitional Federal Government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MostFailedNYer_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-MostFailedNYer-96">[96]</a></sup> Al-Shabaab saw some success in its campaigns against the weak Transitional Federal Government, capturing Baidoa, the base of the Transitional Federal Parliament, on January 26, 2009, and killing three ministers of the government in a December 3, 2009 suicide bomb attack on a medical school graduation ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the drought in 2010, Somalia, including the Al-Shabaab controlled areas, had its best crop yield in seven years. Al-Shabaab claimed some credit for the success, saying that their reduction of oversized cheap food imports allowed Somalia's own grain production, which normally has high potential, to flourish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> They asserted that this policy had the effect of shifting income from urban to rural areas, from mid-income groups to low-income groups, and from overseas farmers to local farmers. However, in response to the drought, Al-Shabaab announced in July 2011 that it had withdrawn its restrictions on international humanitarian workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_News_July_13.2C_2011_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_News_July_13.2C_2011-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, according to the head of the U.N.'s counter-piracy division, Colonel John Steed, Al-Shabaab increasingly sought to cooperate with other criminal organizations and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piracy_in_Somalia" title="Piracy in Somalia">pirate gangs</a> in the face of dwindling funds and resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pirate_links_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-pirate_links-100">[100]</a></sup> Steed, however, acknowledged that he had no definite proof of operational ties between the Islamist militants and the pirates. Detained pirates also indicated to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNODC" title="UNODC">UNODC</a> officials that some measure of cooperation on their part with Al-Shabaab militants was necessary, as they have increasingly launched maritime raids from areas in southern Somalia controlled by the insurgent group. Al-Shabaab members have also extorted the pirates, demanding protection money from them and forcing seized pirate gang leaders in Harardhere to hand over 20% of future ransom proceeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prceuwsm_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prceuwsm-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite routinely expelling, attacking and harassing aid workers, Al-Shabaab permits some agencies to work in areas under its control. At the height of its territorial control it implemented a system of aid agency regulation, taxation and surveillance. Where agencies are allowed to operate, this is often due to the desire of Al-Shabaab to coopt and materially and politically benefit from the provision of aid and services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Senior aid agency representatives often strongly rejected claims that they talked with Al-Shabaab, while aid workers working in Al-Shabaab controlled areas often reported they directly negotiated with the group out of necessity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Al-Shabaab was known as the most prominent terrorist-organization in Somalia which was succeeded to clear away from the bigger cities of the state by the end of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Al-Shabaab has been reduced in power and size since the beginning of the Kenya Army's <a href="/wiki/Operation_Linda_Nchi" title="Operation Linda Nchi">Operation Linda Nchi</a> southern incursion, the group has continued its efforts at recruitment and territorial control. The group maintains training camps in areas near Kismayo in the southern regions of Somalia. One such camp was constructed in Laanta Bur village near Afgooye, which is also where the former K-50 airport is located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> On July 11, 2012, Somali federal troops and their AMISOM allies captured the area from the militants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gafmaiss_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gafmaiss-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition">Opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The U.S. has asserted that al-Shabaab and al-Qaeda pose a global threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-counterterrorism_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-counterterrorism-107">[107]</a></sup> Secretary of Defense <a href="/wiki/Leon_Panetta" title="Leon Panetta">Leon Panetta</a> stated that "U.S. operations against al-Qaida are now concentrating on key groups in Yemen, Somalia and North Africa."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Complaints made against the group include its attacks on aid workers and harsh enforcement of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> law. According to journalist <a href="/wiki/Jon_Lee_Anderson" title="Jon Lee Anderson">Jon Lee Anderson</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The number of people in Somalia who are dependent on international food aid has tripled since 2007, to an estimated 3.6 million. But there is no permanent foreign expatriate presence in southern Somalia, because the Shabaab has declared war on the UN and on Western non-governmental organizations. International relief supplies are flown or shipped into the country and distributed, wherever possible, through local relief workers. Insurgents routinely attack and murder them, too; forty-two have been killed in the past two years alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MostFailedNYer_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-MostFailedNYer-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Shabaab have persecuted Somalia's small <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Somalia" title="Christianity in Somalia">Christian minority</a>, sometimes affixing the label on people they suspect of working for Ethiopian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intelligence_(information_gathering)" title="Intelligence (information gathering)">intelligence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-109">[109]</a></sup> The group has also desecrated the graves of prominent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> Muslims in addition to a Sufi mosque and university, claiming that Sufi practices conflict with their strict interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alertnet.org_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alertnet.org-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-english.aljazeera.net_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-english.aljazeera.net-111">[111]</a></sup> This has led to confrontations with Sufi organized armed groups who have organized under the banner of <a href="/wiki/Ahlu_Sunna_Waljama%27a" title="Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a">Ahlu Sunna Waljama'a</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Somali_rage_at_grave_desecration_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Somali_rage_at_grave_desecration-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Echoing the transition from a nationalistic struggle to one with religious pretenses, Al Shabaab’s propaganda strategy is starting to reflect this shift. Through their religious rhetoric Al Shabaab attempts to recruit and radicalize potential candidates, demoralize their enemies, and dominate dialogue in both national and international media. According to reports Al Shabaab is trying to intensify the conflict: "It would appear from the alleged AMISOM killings that it is determined to portray the war as an affair between Christians and Muslims to shore up support for its fledgling cause... The bodies, some beheaded, were displayed alongside Bibles and crucifixes. The group usually beheads those who have embraced Christianity or Western ideals. Militants have begun placing beheaded corpses next to bibles and crucifixes in order to intimidate local populations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In April 2010 Al Shabaab announced that it would begin banning radio stations from broadcasting BBC and Voice of America, claiming that they were spreading Christian propaganda. By effectively shutting down the Somali media they gain greater control of the dialog surrounding their activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defections">Defections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Defections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2009, Al-Shabaab witnessed a number of its fighters, including several leaders, <a href="/wiki/Defection" title="Defection">defect</a> to Somalia's Transitional Federal Government. One such high-profile defection was that in early November 2009 of Sheikh Mohamed Abdullahi (also known as "Sheikh Bakistani"), who commanded the Maymana Brigade. Sheikh Bakistani told <a href="/wiki/Voice_of_America" title="Voice of America">Voice of America</a> (VOA) Somali Services that he found the group's suicide missions and executions unbearable. He also indicated that his father, a well-known local religious leader, had visited him several times and helped convince him to defect. However, a spokesman for Al-Shabaab denied that Sheikh Bakistani was a member of the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> During the same month, in an interview with <a href="/wiki/Agence_France-Presse" title="Agence France-Presse">Agence France-Presse</a> (AFP) in <a href="/wiki/Villa_Somalia" title="Villa Somalia">Villa Somalia</a> arranged by the Somali federal government, one former Al-Shabaab fighter reported being disillusioned with the group's direction, indicating that while he began fighting in 2006 "to kick out the Ethiopian invaders", he defected a month ago, "disgusted by the false interpretations Al-Shabaab give of Islam". Similarly, a former <a href="/wiki/Hizbul_Islam" title="Hizbul Islam">Hizbul Islam</a> commander recently defected to the Somali government; one of his family members (another Hizbul Islam commander) had been murdered by Al-Shabaab militants as punishment for having escorted a UN convoy. He said in the VOA interview that "if you don't want to fight anymore, there's no point. That's why I quit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> In December 2009, Sheikh Ali Hassan Gheddi, who at the time served as Deputy Commander in-Chief of Al-Shabaab militants in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shabeellaha_Dhexe" title="Shabeellaha Dhexe">Middle Shabele</a> region, also defected to the government, indicating that "Al-Shabaab's cruelty against the people is what forced me to defect to the government side. They extort money from the people and deal with them against the teaching of Islam". Another reason he gave for defecting was Al-Shabaab's then prohibition on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_World_Food_Programme" title="UN World Food Programme">UN World Food Programme</a> (WFP) because he felt that it directly affects civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>With money from extortion dwindling in areas like Mogadishu,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> defections in the face of AMISOM forces, among other internal issues, Al-Shabaab is turning to other militant Islamic groups for support. Al Shabaab has declared their support to bolster their numbers, and has made a number of strategic operational ties to both Al Qaeda and <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Arabian_Peninsula" title="Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula">AQAP</a> in Yemen. In some cases Al Shabaab has begun flying the Al Qeada-Iraq banner at some of its rallies to demonstrate solidarity with the group. There are signs that Al-Shabaab militants are learning from Al Qaeda's propaganda methods. "Shabaab's propaganda has increasingly been slicked up to resemble messages produced by Al Qaeda's 'As-Sahab' ('The Clouds') media wing and AQAP's <i>Inspire</i> magazine, including the release of rap songs by Omar Hammami."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al_Shabaab_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Al_Shabaab-58">[58]</a></sup> It is unclear how the death of AQAP leader Anwar al-Aulaqi and others has affected this bourgeoning relationship between the two. As is evident by their merger with Hizb-ul-Islam in December 2010, Al-Shabaab is turning to former rivals for assistance as their numbers decrease due to defections and casualties directly resulting from battles with AMISOM forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2012, TFG spokesman Abdirahman Omar Osman announced that around 500 militants had already defected from Al-Shabaab to fight alongside government forces. He added that the defections were reportedly increasing on a daily basis since TFG forces had captured the strategically important town of Afgooye from the insurgent group. AMISOM spokesman Lt. Col. Paddy Ankunda similarly indicated that AU commanders were witnessing more defections than at any previous time, a fact he suggested was "a sign al-Shabab is losing cohesion, losing command and control."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Defecting_2012_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Defecting_2012-120">[120]</a></sup> Al-Shabaab's increasingly strident rules, compounded by extortion, harsh punishments, indiscriminate killings and forced conscription of young men and boys, had also reportedly alienated local residents, encouraging a wave of defections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-On_the_run_2012_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-On_the_run_2012-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 5, 2012, a further 200 Al-Shabaab militants and a few senior commanders in Afmadow surrendered to the coalition forces. The defections were interpreted as substantially enhancing the allied offensive since the insurgents could provide details on the Islamist group's combat strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dnsfs_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dnsfs-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 22, 2012, an additional 200 Al-Shabaab insurgents in the town of Garsale near Jowhar surrendered to allied troops. This followed a round of internal battles between rival militants, which left eight of the group's fighters dead, including two top commanders. AMISOM announced in a press statement that it expects the total number of Al-Shabaab defections in the area to reach 250 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aasssaotmdnj_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aasssaotmdnj-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the start of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Indian_Ocean" title="Operation Indian Ocean">Operation Indian Ocean</a> in August 2014, over 700 Al-Shabaab militants have surrendered to the Federal Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alsamstgpa_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alsamstgpa-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 27 December 2014, a Somali intelligence officer indicated that senior Al-Shabaab commander Zakariya Ismail Ahmed Hersi ("Zaki") surrendered to local police in the southwestern Gedo province. According to the official, Hersi may have turned himself in after having fallen out earlier in the year with other Al-Shabaab members loyal to the group's late leader Godane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Welsis_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Welsis-125">[125]</a></sup> On 8 March 2015, the US government officially removed Zaki from its Rewards for Justice List. The decision was reached after negotiations between the Somali federal government and US authorities, which concluded that the former insurgent commander had met the conditions unambiguously establishing that he was no longer associated with the militant group. This in turn came after Zaki had publicly disavowed ties to Al-Shabaab, renounced violence, and fully took part in the peace process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gswugradfrfjl_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gswugradfrfjl-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 17 January 2015, Luq District Police Commissioner Siyad Abdulkadir Mohamed announced that Sheikh Osman Sheikh Mohamed, the commander of Al-Shabaab's militia in the Luq area, had turned himself in to the federal authorities. The rebel leader likewise reportedly handed over all of his weaponry. According to the police official, further Al-Shabaab members intend to defect. He also indicated that the federal government welcomes all former insurgents who disavow of the use of violence and instead pledge to take part in the peace process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gwpcotpotgjf_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gwpcotpotgjf-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 7 March 2015, the Dhusamareeb administration announced that Al-Shabaab landmine expert Abdullahi Mohamed "Madoobe" had surrendered to government forces stationed in the town. According to the local district commissioner Abdirahman Ali Mohamed "Geeda-Qorow" and police commander Abdullahi Garar, the bomb specialist was subsequently put under their protective custody. Garar indicated that Mohamed had also previously trained as a bodyguard. At a press conference, Mohamed concurrently renounced ties with Al-Shabaab, denounced its ideology, and urged young fighters within the militant group to follow suit and defect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Admgdafws_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Admgdafws-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 30, Senior Al-Shabaab officer Bashaan Ali Hassan ("Mohamed Ali") turned himself in to Somali National Army officials in Hudur. According to local residents, the militant leader had served in the insurgent group's Bakool and Lower Shabelle province contingents. SNA commander in Bakool Abdirahman Mohamed Osman "Tima-Adde" indicated that the government forces were conducting a probe to ascertain the circumstances surrounding Hassan's surrender. He also hailed the defection as a major setback for Al-Shabaab and its leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gsaostfg_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gsaostfg-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategy">Strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table cellspacing="5" class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; width: 25em; max-width: 25%;">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;"><small>Chorus:</small>
<p><small>"Send me a cruise like Maa’lam Adam al Ansari</small></p>
<p><small>And send me a couple of tons like Zarqawi</small></p>
<p><small>And send me a drone like Abu Layth al Libi</small></p>
<p><small>And Special Forces like Saalih an Nabhani."</small></p>
<p><small>"Send me all four and send me much much more</small></p>
<p><small>I pray for that on my way to heavens door</small></p>
<p><small>Send me four and send me more, that what I implore</small></p>
<p><small>An amazing martyrdom I strive for and adore."</small></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: right;">— "Send Me A Cruise"<br/>
by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansoor_Al-Amriki" title="Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki">Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asbohsmac_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asbohsmac-130">[130]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Al-Shabaab proliferates their propaganda through various media. It operates its own radio station, Radio Andalus, and has acquired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relay_station" title="Relay station">relay stations</a> and seized other equipment from private radio stations, including some from the <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">BBC</a>. Presenters broadcast in Somali, Arabic, Swahili and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-media_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-media-131">[131]</a></sup> Besides radio, the Internet is the most heavily utilized by Al-Shabaab and other militant Islamic groups such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> because it is the easiest and most cost-effective way to reach a large audience. As the internet is especially popular with today's youth, organizations such as Al-Shabaab are using online forums and chat rooms to recruit young followers. Al-Shabaab's official website, which has since been taken down, featured posts, videos and official statements in English, Arabic and Somali, as well as online classrooms to educate followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Prior to its expulsion from Mogadishu in mid-2011, Al-Shabaab had also launched the Al-Kataib propaganda television station the year before. The channel's pilot program aired the confessions of Ahmed Kisi, an alleged CIA spy who had been executed earlier in the week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, Al-Shabaab also uses music to influence and appeal to young followers. According to Robin Wright, "By 2010, almost eight out of every ten soldiers in Somalia's many rebel forces were children," who are especially influenced and susceptible messages conveyed to modern, western-themed music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> One of Al Shabaab's foreign-born leaders, American Omar Hammami aka <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansoor_Al-Amriki" title="Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki">Abu Mansoor Al-Amriki</a>, gained notoriety after an April 2009 video of him rapping about jihad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Hammami's most recent song, "Send Me a Cruise", debuted online on April 9, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asbohsmac_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asbohsmac-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2013 Al-Shabaab issued a propaganda video targeting several British Muslims who had spoken out against Islamist extremism, some of them explicitly against the <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Lee_Rigby" title="Murder of Lee Rigby">murder of Lee Rigby</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aljazeera_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera-136">[136]</a></sup> The video urged jihadists in the UK to follow the example of Rigby's killers, to arm themselves if necessary with knives from B&amp;Q.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aljazeera_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera-136">[136]</a></sup> The Muslims named in the video for "selling out"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ManchesterDeath_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-ManchesterDeath-137">[137]</a></sup> included <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Shafiq" title="Mohammed Shafiq">Mohammed Shafiq</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammed_Ansar" title="Mohammed Ansar">Mohammed Ansar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Usama_Hasan" title="Usama Hasan">Usama Hasan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ajmal_Masroor" title="Ajmal Masroor">Ajmal Masroor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aljazeera_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2015, Al-Shabaab released another propaganda video calling for attacks on shopping malls in the U.S., Canada and the U.K., including the <a href="/wiki/West_Edmonton_Mall" title="West Edmonton Mall">West Edmonton Mall</a> in Alberta, Canada and the <a href="/wiki/Mall_of_America" title="Mall of America">Mall of America</a> in Bloomington, Minnesota, U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nittwemvrs_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nittwemvrs-138">[138]</a></sup> Although the group had hitherto only ever launched attacks within East Africa, security at both malls was tightened in response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a> also indicated that there was no evidence of any imminent threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nittwemvrs_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nittwemvrs-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Twitter_account">Twitter account</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Twitter account">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On December 7, 2011, Al-Shabaab also reportedly began using the Twitter social media network. The move is believed to be an attempt by the group to counteract tweets by allied officials, and to serve as a venue for the dissemination of information on alleged casualties as well as a way to interact with the press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The account, HSMPress, has attracted over eight thousand followers for its witty taunts of the KDF in general and its official spokesman, Maj. Emmanuel Chirchir, with whom it has frequent exchanges, in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_HSMPress_story_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_HSMPress_story-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, after Chirchir upbraided the Shabaab for not letting women in the areas under their control wear bras, saying life had more to offer, HSMPress retorted "Like bombing donkeys, you mean!", referring to a recent announcement by Chirchir that any large group of loaded donkeys would be considered a target. "Your eccentric battle strategy has got animal rights groups quite concerned, Major."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HSMPress_bombing_donkeys_tweet_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-HSMPress_bombing_donkeys_tweet-142">[142]</a></sup> Later, responding to Chirchir's claim that Kismayo had been captured by the KDF, HSMPress said the Kenyan "boys are a grotesque parody of an army! They can outpace ur world-class runners by far. Indeed, they 'Run like a Kenyan'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HSMPress_Kenyan_running_tweet_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-HSMPress_Kenyan_running_tweet-143">[143]</a></sup> The account shows a less belligerent side with others, telling a UN official who queried "it is good when extremists or perceived extremists come out and talk[..] can we have a coffee with them too?" that "a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caramel_macchiato" title="Caramel macchiato">caramel macchiato</a> would do!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HSMPress_caramel_macchiato_tweet_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-HSMPress_caramel_macchiato_tweet-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>While it is not known for certain if the HSMPress account is sanctioned by the Shabaab, both Western and African Union officials believe that it is. It has relayed information about battle outcomes that has sometimes been more accurate than its opponents, and posted pictures of authentic identity cards of missing AMISOM peacekeepers that were presumably killed in combat. The account itself is operated by a man with the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nom_de_guerre" title="Nom de guerre">nom de guerre</a></i> Sheik Yoonis, who has in the past responded to press questions during telephone interviews in a "clipped British accent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_HSMPress_story_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_HSMPress_story-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of Al-Shabaab's messages on Twitter are in English, with authorities suggesting that they are intended for an outside audience and potential recruits in the West. Officials in the United States, where Twitter is based, are exploring legal ways to terminate the account, although they acknowledge that doing so might raise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_speech" title="Free speech">free speech</a> concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_official_concerns_about_Twitter_story_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_official_concerns_about_Twitter_story-145">[145]</a></sup> Chirchir commented in a tweet of his own that such a move would be counterproductive, as "Al Shabaab needs to be engaged positively and twitter is the only avenue".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirchir_response_tweet_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chirchir_response_tweet-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2013, Twitter suspended Al-Shabaab's English account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sastasa_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sastasa-148">[148]</a></sup> This was apparently in response to the account having issued death threats against Frenchman "Denis Allex" and subsequently posted photos of his corpse after the botched <a href="/wiki/Bulo_Marer_hostage_rescue_attempt" title="Bulo Marer hostage rescue attempt">Bulo Marer hostage rescue attempt</a>, as well as tweeting threats to kill Kenyan hostages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sastasa_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sastasa-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aqgibotab_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aqgibotab-149">[149]</a></sup> Al-Shabaab later opened a new Twitter account on February 4, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aqgibotab_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aqgibotab-149">[149]</a></sup> Twitter closed the account again on September 6, 2013 for unspecified reasons. A few days earlier, on September 3, the insurgent group had used the service to claim responsibility for an unsuccessful ambush attempt against a convoy carrying Somalian President <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Sheikh_Mohamud" title="Hassan Sheikh Mohamud">Hassan Sheikh Mohamud</a>. The militants also tweeted after the attack that the group had no other active Twitter feeds in English, and cautioned users against "parody accounts". The insurgent group also messaged that "next time, you won't be as lucky," in apparent violation of Twitter's user policies against issuing threats of violence and using the service for illicit purposes or activities. However, Al-Shabaab's Arabic account remained open.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sastasa_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sastasa-148">[148]</a></sup> The group later relaunched its English Twitter account on September 11, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asasbotas_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asasbotas-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2013, Twitter suspended at least six Al-Shabaab accounts after the outfit ridiculed the Kenyan government's response to the <a href="/wiki/Westgate_shopping_mall_attack" title="Westgate shopping mall attack">Westgate shopping mall attack</a> in Nairobi, an attack Al-Shabaab claimed responsibility for. The group later re-opened a Twitter account in December, with the explanation that "the aim is to vigorously challenge defamatory reports in the media by presenting an accurate portrayal of the current state of Jihad in Somalia and countering Western, state-sponsored propaganda machines that are paid to demonise the Mujahideen." A Somali government spokesman stated that the Somali authorities were opposed to Al-Shabaab's presence on the social media website, as the group "should not be given the platform to mislead the youth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asstabot_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asstabot-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drought">Drought</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Drought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Eastern_Africa_drought" title="2011 Eastern Africa drought">2011 Eastern Africa drought</a>, Al Shabaab adapted its propaganda strategy to accommodate the changing circumstances. In some cases, group members employed humanitarian aid as a recruitment tool, using relief supplies as bribes and as an incentive to join the militants, whose numbers had decreased due to casualties and defections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Group members dismissed the UN declaration of famine in various regions as grossly exaggerated and banned various organizations from providing aid to those regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response, the Prime Minister of Somalia <a href="/wiki/Abdiweli_Mohamed_Ali" title="Abdiweli Mohamed Ali">Abdiweli Mohamed Ali</a> in July 2011 appointed a national committee to tackle the severe drought affecting the southern part of the country,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rrsgnndc_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rrsgnndc-154">[154]</a></sup> and the following month announced the creation of a new 300-man security force. Assisted by African Union peacekeepers, the military unit had as its primary goal to protect convoys and aid from the Al-Shabaab rebels, as well as to secure the IDP camps when the relief supplies are being distributed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pmasuapf_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pmasuapf-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although fighting disrupted aid delivery in some areas, a scaling up of relief operations in mid-November prompted the UN to downgrade the humanitarian situation in several regions from famine to emergency levels. Humanitarian access to Al-Shabaab-controlled areas had also improved and rainfall had surpassed expectations, improving the prospects of a good harvest in early 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rfrisbwba_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rfrisbwba-156">[156]</a></sup> In February 2012, the UN declares that Somalia has produced a bumper harvest, and that the famine is over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operation_Linda_Nchi">Operation Linda Nchi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Operation Linda Nchi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Somali_Police_Force.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="217" data-file-width="326" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flag_of_the_Somali_Police_Force.gif/220px-Flag_of_the_Somali_Police_Force.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Flag_of_the_Somali_Police_Force.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Somali_Police_Force.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Somalia" title="Military of Somalia">Somali National Army</a> (SNA), <a href="/wiki/Somali_Police_Force" title="Somali Police Force">Somali Police Force</a> (SPF) and their allies have intensified security operations against Al-Shabaab.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the TFG-led <a href="/wiki/Operation_Linda_Nchi" title="Operation Linda Nchi">Operation Linda Nchi</a> between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Somalia" title="Military of Somalia">Somalian National Army</a> (SNA) and the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Defence_Forces" title="Kenya Defence Forces">Kenya Defence Forces</a> (KDF) against Al-Shabaab militants in southern Somalia began,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jointc_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jointc-158">[158]</a></sup> Al Shabaab has been intensifying its propaganda effort – a signal perhaps that militant forces is growing desperate as it suffers heavy losses. Group members have started to diversify their tactics, using various methods to demoralize the allied forces. According to the Associated Press, Al Shabaab has resorted to dressing up some of its own casualties in TFG and AU uniforms, although an African Union spokesman indicated that only two corpses of AU soldiers were unaccounted for. About half of the dead bodies were also visibly Somali, prompting eyewitnesses to suggest that they were fallen Somali government soldiers. The remainder were dressed in Burundi military uniforms and resembled non-Somali foreigners, with Al-Shabaab militants displaying a Bible and some crucifixes reportedly taken from the deceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Additionally, Al-Shabaab has been conducting militia parades as a show of force in cities such as Marka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Al Shabaab is suffering heavy military losses, the effectiveness of their propaganda campaign to date is somewhat inconclusive. What is apparent, however, is that they are increasing their propaganda efforts without corresponding response from TFG, AMISOM and KDF forces. Al-Shabaab retreats from regions in southern Somalia and areas around Mogadishu are falsely heralded as tactical maneuvers by the militants who are facing defeat – while the allied forces remain largely muted on the success that they have made in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>The propaganda techniques employed by Al-Shabaab show the stark contrast between militant forces and the conventional armies of AMISOM. While Shabaab forces act with impunity in regards to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guerrilla_tactics" title="Guerrilla tactics">guerrilla tactics</a>, the allied forces are obligated to comply with articles of the Geneva Convention that require them to warn civilians of air raids and troop movements – oftentimes informing the very militants they intend to strike and leaving them unable to act when they observe flagrant militant activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Jazeera" title="Al-Jazeera">Al-Jazeera</a>, Al-Shabaab have also attempted to capitalize on the coordinated incursion by depicting itself as a resistance force fighting foreign occupiers and urged local residents to take up arms against the Kenyan soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aljazeera_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aljazeera-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_other_militant_groups">Relations with other militant groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Relations with other militant groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Al-Qaeda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On February 9, 2012, Mukhtar Abu al-Zubair 'Godane' announced in a fifteen-minute video message that Al-Shabaab would be joining the militant Islamist organization <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a>, under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a>. Al-Zubair stated, "On behalf of the soldiers and the commanders in al-Shabaab, we pledge allegiance to you. So lead us to the path of <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shahid" title="Shahid">martyrdom</a> that was drawn by our imam, the martyr <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlQaeda_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-AlQaeda-7">[7]</a></sup> Al-Zawahiri approved and welcomed Al-Shabaab as al-Qaeda's Somalia-based affiliate in a 15-minute video response, stating "Today, I have glad tidings for the <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a> that will please the believers and disturb the disbelievers, which is the joining of the Shabaab al-Mujahideen Movement in Somalia to Qaeda al-Jihad, to support the jihadi unity against the Zio-Crusader campaign and their assistants amongst the treacherous agent rulers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The merger follows reports about a rift in the leadership,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> and it coincides with reports about large factions breaking away from Al Shabaab,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> and up to 500 Al Shabaab fighters fleeing or leaving southern Somalia for <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> where a full Al Qaeda branch <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Arabian_Peninsula" title="Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula">AQAP</a> is stepping up operations, under perceived increased military pressure since a new president took office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Somalia's Transitional Federal Government officially recognized the two Islamist groups as one group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SLVTCCOAQ_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-SLVTCCOAQ-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>A poll conducted between 8–16 April 2012 by the international market research company <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> examined the views of MENA region residents with regard to the news of the merger. The combined group evoked fear in most respondents, with 42% believing that the merger announcement ought to be a source of alarm for the international community; 23% of polltakers felt very strongly about this. 45% of respondents believed that the fusion of the two groups would enhance Al-Qaeda's attempts at recruiting new operatives, with 12% indicating that the merger would strengthen the latter group's capabilities and another 11% believing that it would result in more terrorist attacks on the continent. A further 55% of pollsters did not know how the Somalian leadership would respond to news of the merger, though 36% suggested that it would lead to more movements against Al-Shabaab by the Somalian military. 34% of respondents also indicated that announcement of the merger constituted a propaganda effort aimed at securing more coverage for the two Islamist groups, with 30% of polltakers believing that the decision to merge shows that both Al-Shabaab and Al-Qaeda are under duress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-You_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-You-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to Godane's announced name change and merger with al-Qaeda, all other Shabaab top leaders called a conference in Baidabo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> They refused to adopt the new name (al-Qaeda in East Africa) and they agreed on a new policy, focusing entirely on domestic issues and with no mention any more of international struggle. One significant policy proposal was to form a national, independent Shura of Islamic clerics, which means also independent of al-Qaeda. With it, they seem to try to remove some obstacles for reaching an entente with their Sufi opponents, and to avoid getting targeted by US drones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Aweys later declared that: "Al-Shabaab and al-Qaeda are merely a small part of the larger Islamic group and al-Qaeda's ideology should not be viewed as the sole, righteous path for Islam."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>This open revolt against al-Qaeda made it more likely that Al-Shabaab would slowly become ready for some sort of negotiated entente.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> On February 23, 2012, while Shabaab was pushed out of several strongholds, Radio Magadishu reported that 120 al-Qaeda leaders and followers fled from Kismayo to Yemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Aweys was appointed military commander of Kismayo and the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2013, the internal rifts within Al-Shabaab erupted into all-out warfare between Godane's faction and those of other leaders in the organization. In late June, four senior Shabaab commanders were executed under the orders of Godane. One of these commanders was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibrahim_al-Afghani" title="Ibrahim al-Afghani">Ibrahim al-Afghani</a>, who had complained about the leadership style of Godane in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a>. Sixteen others were arrested, and Aweys fled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> He was later taken into custody in Mogadishu by Somali government forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sfailim_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sfailim-179">[179]</a></sup> On 12 September, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omar_Hammami" title="Omar Hammami">Omar Hammami</a>, who had left the group due to significant disagreements with Godane, was killed by Al-Shabaab forces. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westgate_shopping_mall_shooting" title="Westgate shopping mall shooting">Westgate shopping mall shooting</a> in September was said by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Tisdall" title="Simon Tisdall">Simon Tisdall</a> to be a reflection of the power struggle within the insurgent group, with Godane's hardline global jihadi faction seeking to exert its authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="AQIM_and_Boko_Haram">AQIM and Boko Haram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: AQIM and Boko Haram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to U.S. Army General Carter Ham, Al-Shabaab, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_Islamic_Maghreb" title="Al-Qaeda in Islamic Maghreb">Al-Qaeda in Islamic Maghreb</a> (AQIM) and the Nigeria-based <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram" title="Boko Haram">Boko Haram</a> (BH) were as of June 2012 attempting to synchronize and coordinate their activities in terms of sharing funds, training and explosives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atgstcus_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atgstcus-181">[181]</a></sup> Ham added that he believed that the collaboration presented a threat to both U.S. homeland security and the local authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aqbhasm_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aqbhasm-182">[182]</a></sup> However, according to counter-terrorism specialist Rick Nelson with the Washington-based Center for Strategic International Studies, there was little evidence that the three groups were targeting U.S. areas, as each was primarily interested in establishing fundamentalist administrations in their respective regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atgstcus_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atgstcus-181">[181]</a></sup> In May 2014, Senior Al-Shabab member <a href="/wiki/Fuad_Qalaf" title="Fuad Qalaf">Fuad Shongole</a> stated that al-Shabab fighters would carry out <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a>, or holy war, in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> "and afterward, with God's will, to America."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hizbul_Islam">Hizbul Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hizbul Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On September 24, 2012, <a href="/wiki/Hizbul_Islam" title="Hizbul Islam">Hizbul Islam</a> spokesman Mohamed Moallim announced that his group was discontinuing its association with Al-Shabaab, a group that he asserted his organization had only nominally united with. Moallim cited the significant political changes happening in Somalia as well as Al-Shabaab's reported issuance of propaganda against Hizbul Islam as the primary reasons for his group's decision to leave the coalition. He added that his organization did not share Al-Shabaab's political philosophy, and that he felt the militant group had been considerably "weakened". Moallim also indicated that Hizbul Islam was open to talks with any political actors in the country working for a common good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gshigwasasiw_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gshigwasasiw-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shiswas_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shiswas-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Starting in early 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL) released a series of videos online aimed at al-Shabaab, calling on the group to switch allegiances from al-Qaeda to ISIL’s leader, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Baghdadi" title="Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi">Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> By September 2015, Al-Shabab issued an internal memo aimed at pro-ISIL elements in its ranks, stating that the group’s policy is to continue its allegiance to al-Qaida, and banned any discussion relating to ISIL. The group also detained some of its fighters who had voiced support for ISIL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2015, senior al-Shabaab commander <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Qadir_Mumin" title="Abdul Qadir Mumin">Abdul Qadir Mumin</a> and approximately 20 of his followers in the Puntland region pledged allegiance to ISIL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Further defections in al-Shabaab ranks occurred in the border region between Somalia and Northern Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> In November 2015, a pro-ISIL commander called Hussein Abdi Gedi was ambushed and killed, and at least 9 al-Shabaab fighters were killed in fighting between the two factions. The head of al-Shabab in the Lower Shabelle region, Abu Abdalla, gave an interview in which he said that all pro-ISIL members should leave the group or be killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voa_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-voa-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bounties">Bounties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bounties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2012, the United States government began a new policy of offering financial rewards in exchange for information as to the whereabouts of Al-Shabaab members. On June 7, the U.S. Department of State put forth an offer totaling $33 million for the capture of seven of Al-Shabaab's senior commanders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arsasotcfoc_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arsasotcfoc-191">[191]</a></sup> including a reported $3–$7 million (£2–£4.5 million) per leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smsascrfboa_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smsascrfboa-15">[15]</a></sup> $7 million of the total funds were set aside for information regarding the insurgent group's Amir or Spiritual Leader, Ahmed Godane (Abu Zubayr), with another $5 million bounty on Al-Shabaab's Deputy Leader, Mukhtar Robow (Abu Mansur).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arsasotcfoc_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arsasotcfoc-191">[191]</a></sup> Additionally, a $3 million bounty was reserved for the senior commander Zakariya Ismail Ahmed Hersi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Welsis_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Welsis-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 8, Somalia's Transitional Federal Government (TFG) released an official statement expressing support for the initiative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arsasotcfoc_191-2"><a href="#cite_note-Arsasotcfoc-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response, senior Al-Shabaab commander Fu'ad Mohamed Khalaf (Sheikh Shongole) issued a mock offer of his own the same day, promising 10 camels to anyone possessing information on U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>. Shongole also mockingly offered a less valuable bounty of 10 cocks and 10 hens for information concerning American Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arsasotcfoc_191-3"><a href="#cite_note-Arsasotcfoc-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>During an official state visit to Mogadishu, top U.S. envoy <a href="/wiki/Johnnie_Carson" title="Johnnie Carson">Johnnie Carson</a> dismissed Al-Shabaab's counter-offer as "absurd". He also indicated that the American government would impose sanctions on anyone attempting to thwart the ongoing political process, including invoking visa and travel bans and freezing assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smsascrfboa_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smsascrfboa-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 21, 2013, the U.S. Department of State announced another bounty of $5 million apiece for information on two American senior Al-Shabaab commanders, Abu Mansour al-Amriki (Omar Shafik Hammami) and Jehad Serwan Mostafa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uspboamis_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uspboamis-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 15, 2014, the U.S. Department of State also began offering bounties of up to $3 million apiece for information leading to the arrest or conviction of the Al-Shabaab senior members Abdikadir Mohamed Abdikadir, Yasin Kilwe and Jafar. According to State Department officials, Abdikadir coordinates Al-Shabaab's recruitment activities in Kenya, with Jafar acting as his deputy; Kilwe serves as Al-Shabaab's Emir for the northeastern Puntland region. The bounties are part of the "Rewards for Justice" program, wherein money is issued for leads on terror suspects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guotbftseltaq_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guotbftseltaq-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 27, 2014, the <a href="/wiki/National_Intelligence_and_Security_Agency" title="National Intelligence and Security Agency">National Intelligence and Security Agency</a> (NISA) offered a $2 million reward to any individual who provides information leading to the arrest of the new Al-Shabaab leader, Ahmed Omar Abu Ubeyda. According to the NISA Commander <a href="/wiki/Abdurahman_Mohamud_Turyare" title="Abdurahman Mohamud Turyare">Abdirahman Mohamed Turyare</a>, a separate $1 million would be rewarded to any person who supplies information that could result in the killing of Ubeyda. Turyare also pledged that the informers' identities would be kept private. This is reportedly the first time that a Somalia security official is offering such large dead-or-alive bounties on an Al-Shabaab leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sgpboasl_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sgpboasl-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 3, 2015, the Kenyan government offered a 20 million <a href="/wiki/Kenyan_shilling" title="Kenyan shilling">Kenyan shillings</a> ($215,000) reward for the arrest of Mohamed Mohamud, who serves as a commander of Al-Shabaab operations in Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asswkpolgw_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asswkpolgw-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 10, 2015, the Federal Government of Somalia offered a $250,000 reward for the capture of Al-Shabaab commander Ahmed Diriye. It also placed bounties of between $100,000 to $150,000 for information on the whereabouts or leading to the arrest of several other of the militant group's leaders, including Mahad Warsame Galay (Mahad Karate), Ali Mohamed Raage (Ali Dhere), Abdullahi Abdi (Daud Suheyb), Mohamed Mohamud Noor “Sultan”, Ali Mohamed Hussein (Ali Jeesto), Mohamed Mohamud (Gama-Dhere), Hassan Mohamed Afgoye, Mohamed Abdi Muse Mohamed, Yasin Osman Kilwa and Abdullahi Osman. Additionally, the federal government indicated that any leads forwarded to it vis-a-vis the wanted insurgent commanders would be kept strictly confidential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gsgpboasl_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gsgpboasl-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_allegations">Support allegations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Support allegations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eritrea">Eritrea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Eritrea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In December 2009, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> imposed sanctions on <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, accusing the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> country of arming and providing financial aid to militia groups in southern Somalia's conflict zones, including al-Shabaab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erunribsr_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erunribsr-196">[196]</a></sup> Plane loads of weapons said to be coming from Eritrea were sent to anti-government rebels in southern Somalia. AU peacekeepers also reportedly captured some Eritrean soldiers and prisoners of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> In 2010, the UN International Monitoring Group (IMG) also published a report charging the Eritrean government of continuing to offer support to rebel groups in southern Somalia, despite the sanctions already placed on the nation. The Eritrean administration emphatically denied the accusations, describing them as "concocted, baseless and unfounded" and demanding concrete evidence to be made publicly available, with an independent platform through which it may in turn issue a response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erunribsr_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erunribsr-196">[196]</a></sup> In November 2011 the UN Monitoring Group repeated claims that Eritrea would support al-Shabaab. The report says that Eritrea gives <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>80,000 each month to al-Shabaab linked individuals in Nairobi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 5, 2012 the Obama administration announced sanctions on Eritrea's intelligence chief and on a high-ranking military officer related to allegations of their support of Al-Shabaab. Col. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tewolde_Habte_Negash&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tewolde Habte Negash (page does not exist)">Tewolde Habte Negash</a> is accused of providing training and support while Col. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Taeme_Abraham_Goitom&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Taeme Abraham Goitom (page does not exist)">Taeme Abraham Goitom</a> is alleged to organize armed opposition to the Somalian government. The sanctions freeze any of the individual's U.S. assets and prohibits Americans from conducting business with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo20120705_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo20120705-200">[200]</a></sup> On July 16, 2012, a United Nations Monitoring Group report stated that "it had found no evidence of direct Eritrean support for al Shabaab in the past year."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Somaliland">Somaliland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Somaliland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2010, reports surfaced linking the secessionist government of the northwestern <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a> region with the Islamist extremists that are currently waging war against the Transitional Federal Government and its African Union allies. The <a href="/wiki/International_Strategic_Studies_Association" title="International Strategic Studies Association">International Strategic Studies Association</a> (ISSA) published several reports shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Somaliland_presidential_election,_2010" title="Somaliland presidential election, 2010">2010 presidential elections</a> in Somaliland, accusing the enclave's newly elected president <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmed_M._Mahamoud_Silanyo" title="Ahmed M. Mahamoud Silanyo">Ahmed M. Mahamoud Silanyo</a> of having strong ties with Islamist groups, and suggesting that his political party <a href="/wiki/Peace,_Unity,_and_Development_Party" title="Peace, Unity, and Development Party">Kulmiye</a> won the election in large part due to support from a broad-based network of Islamists, including al-Shabaab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hrsbfiasmtirws_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hrsbfiasmtirws-202">[202]</a></sup> The ISSA also described Dr. Mohamed Abdi Gaboose, Somaliland's new Interior Minister, as an Islamist with "strong personal connections with al-Shabaab", and predicted that the militant group would consequently be empowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spboastgeemr_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spboastgeemr-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2011, <a href="/wiki/Puntland" title="Puntland">Puntland</a> accused <a href="/wiki/Somaliland" title="Somaliland">Somaliland</a> of providing a safe haven for <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Said_Atom" title="Mohamed Said Atom">Mohamed Said Atom</a>, an arms smuggler believed to be allied with al-Shabaab. Somaliland strenuously denied the charges, calling them a smokescreen to divert attention from Puntland's own activities.</p>
<p>Atom and his men were reportedly hiding out and receiving medical attention in Somaliland after being <a href="/wiki/Galgala_campaign" title="Galgala campaign">pursued by Puntland forces</a> in late 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garowe_Online_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garowe_Online-204">[204]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Puntland_Intelligence_Agency" title="Puntland Intelligence Agency">Puntland Intelligence Agency</a> also claimed that over 70 Somaliland soldiers had fought alongside Atom's militiamen, including one known intelligence official who died in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Somaliland media reported in January that Atom's representative requested military assistance from the Somaliland authorities, and that he denied that Atom's militia was linked to al-Shabaab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>Puntland government documents claim that Atom's militia were used as proxy agents in 2006. They accuse Somaliland of offering financial and military assistance to destabilize Puntland and distract attention from attempts to occupy the disputed <a href="/wiki/Sool,_Somalia" title="Sool, Somalia">Sool</a> province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garowe_Online_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garowe_Online-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kenyan forces have been working hand in hand to fight the war against Al Shabaab in the region. The total death toll on Al Shabaab is 1,392 since 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
